<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Biodiversity</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Variety and variability of life forms</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/220px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/330px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG/440px-Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fungi_of_Saskatchewan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sampling of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> collected during summer 2008 in Northern <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> mixed woods, near LaRonge, is an example regarding the species diversity of fungus. In this photo, there are also leaf <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Biodiversity</b> or <b>biological diversity</b> is the variety and variability of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>. Biodiversity is a measure of variation at the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Genetic_variability" title="Genetic variability">genetic variability</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a></i>), and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_diversity" title="Ecosystem diversity">ecosystem diversity</a></i>) level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biodiversity is not distributed evenly on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, it is usually greater in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> as a result of the warm <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_productivity" title="Primary productivity">primary productivity</a> in the region near the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. These tropical forest ecosystems cover less than 10% of earth's surface and contain about 90% of the world's species. <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">Marine biodiversity</a> is usually higher along coasts in the Western <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperature</a> is highest, and in the mid-latitudinal band in all oceans. There are <a href="/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity" title="Latitudinal gradients in species diversity">latitudinal gradients in species diversity</a>. Biodiversity generally tends to cluster in <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">hotspots</a>, and has been increasing through time, but will be likely to slow in the future as a primary result of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>. It encompasses the evolutionary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">ecological</a>, and cultural processes that sustain life.
</p><p>Rapid <a href="/wiki/Environmental_change" title="Environmental change">environmental changes</a> typically cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a>. More than 99.9% of all species that ever lived on Earth, amounting to over five billion species, are estimated to be <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>. Estimates on the number of Earth's current <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> range from 10 million to 14 million, of which about 1.2 million have been documented and over 86% have not yet been described. The total amount of related <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> on Earth is estimated at 5.0 x 10<sup>37</sup> and weighs 50 billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>. In comparison, the total <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">mass</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> has been estimated to be as much as four trillion tons of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>. In July 2016, scientists reported identifying a set of 355 <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Universal_Common_Ancestor" title="Last Universal Common Ancestor">Last Universal Common Ancestor</a> (LUCA) of all <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> living on Earth.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a> is about 4.54 billion years. The earliest undisputed evidence of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a> dates at least from 3.5 billion years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Eoarchean" title="Eoarchean">Eoarchean</a> Era after a geological <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> started to solidify following the earlier molten <a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean">Hadean</a> Eon. There are <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> discovered in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>. Other early physical evidence of a <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic substance</a> is <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> in 3.7 billion-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">meta-sedimentary rocks</a> discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a>. More recently, in 2015, "remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic life</a>" were found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia. According to one of the researchers, "If life arose relatively quickly on Earth...then it could be common in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apnews.excite.com_2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-apnews.excite.com_2015-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">life began on Earth</a>, five major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinctions" title="Mass extinctions">mass extinctions</a> and several minor events have led to large and sudden drops in biodiversity. The <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> aeon (the last 540 million years) marked a rapid growth in biodiversity via the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>—a period during which the majority of <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> first appeared. The next 400 million years included repeated, massive <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity losses</a> classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> events. In the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous_rainforest_collapse" title="Carboniferous rainforest collapse">rainforest collapse</a> led to a great loss of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> life. The <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, 251 million years ago, was the worst; vertebrate recovery took 30 million years. The most recent, the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, occurred 65 million years ago and has often attracted more attention than others because it resulted in the extinction of the <span class="nowrap">non-avian</span> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>.
</p><p>The period since the emergence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> has displayed an ongoing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity reduction</a> and an accompanying loss of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>. Named the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a>, and often referred to as the sixth mass extinction, the reduction is caused primarily by <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impacts</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a> destruction. Conversely, biodiversity positively impacts <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">human health</a> in many ways, although a few negative effects are studied.
</p>

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_and_etymology">Naming and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Naming and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>1916 – The term <i>biological diversity</i> was used first by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._Arthur_Harris" title="J. Arthur Harris">J. Arthur Harris</a> in "The Variable Desert," <i><a href="/wiki/Scientific_American" title="Scientific American">Scientific American</a></i>: "The bare statement that the region contains a flora rich in genera and species and of diverse geographic origin or affinity is entirely inadequate as a description of its real biological diversity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
<li>1967 - <a href="/wiki/Raymond_F._Dasmann" title="Raymond F. Dasmann">Raymond F. Dasmann</a> used the term biological diversity in reference to the richness of living nature that conservationists should protect in his book A Different Kind of Country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li>1974 – The term <i>natural diversity</i> was introduced by <a href="/wiki/John_Terborgh" title="John Terborgh">John Terborgh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li>1980 – <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lovejoy" title="Thomas Lovejoy">Thomas Lovejoy</a> introduced the term <i>biological diversity</i> to the scientific community in a book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConsBiol80_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConsBiol80-7">[7]</a></sup> It rapidly became commonly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>1985 – According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_O._Wilson" title="Edward O. Wilson">Edward O. Wilson</a>, the contracted form <i>biodiversity</i> was coined by W. G. Rosen: "The National Forum on BioDiversity ... was conceived by Walter G.Rosen ... Dr. Rosen represented the NRC/NAS throughout the planning stages of the project. Furthermore, he introduced the term <i>biodiversity</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>1985 - The term "biodiversity" appears in the article, "A New Plan to Conserve the Earth's Biota" by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Laura_Tangley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Laura Tangley (page does not exist)">Laura Tangley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li>1988 - The term biodiversity first appeared in publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilsonSciences1988_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WilsonSciences1988-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li>The present – the term has achieved widespread use.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2022)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Biodiversity" is most commonly used to replace the more clearly-defined and long-established terms, <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Species_richness" title="Species richness">species richness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
Biologists most often define biodiversity as the "totality of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> of a region".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larsson2001_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larsson2001-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-15">[15]</a></sup> An advantage of this definition is that it presents a unified view of the traditional types of biological variety previously identified:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomic_diversity" title="Taxonomic diversity">taxonomic diversity</a> (usually measured at the species diversity level)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_diversity" title="Ecological diversity">ecological diversity</a> (often viewed from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_diversity" title="Ecosystem diversity">ecosystem diversity</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li>morphological diversity (which stems from <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular diversity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Functional_group_(ecology)" title="Functional group (ecology)">functional diversity</a> (which is a measure of the number of functionally disparate species within a population (e.g. different feeding mechanism, different motility, predator vs prey, etc.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>) This multilevel construct is consistent with Datman and Lovejoy.</li></ul>
<p>Other definitions include:
</p>
<dl><dt>Wilcox 1982</dt>
<dd>An explicit definition consistent with this interpretation was first given in a paper by Bruce A. Wilcox commissioned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature_and_Natural_Resources" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources">International Union for the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources</a> (IUCN) for the 1982 World National Parks Conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcox84_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilcox84-19">[19]</a></sup> Wilcox's definition was "Biological diversity is the variety of life forms...at all levels of biological systems (i.e., molecular, organismic, population, species and ecosystem)...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcox84_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilcox84-19">[19]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt>Genetic</dt>
<dd>Wilcox 1984</dd>
<dd>Biodiversity can be defined genetically as the diversity of alleles, genes and <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. They study processes such as <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gene_transfer" title="Gene transfer">gene transfer</a> that drive evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilcox84_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-wilcox84-19">[19]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt>United Nations 1992</dt>
<dd>The 1992 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">Earth Summit</a> defined "biological diversity" as "the variability among living organisms from all sources, including, <i>inter alia</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecoregion" title="Terrestrial ecoregion">terrestrial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">aquatic ecosystems</a> and the ecological complexes of which they are part: this includes diversity within species, between species and of ecosystems".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth1996_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth1996-20">[20]</a></sup> This definition is used in the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth1996_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth1996-20">[20]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt>Gaston and Spicer 2004</dt>
<dd>Gaston &amp; Spicer's definition in their book "Biodiversity: an introduction" is "variation of life at all levels of biological organization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GastonSpicer2004_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GastonSpicer2004-21">[21]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<dl><dt>Food and Agriculture Organization 2019</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the United Nations (FAO) defines biodiversity as "the variability that exists among living organisms (both within and between species) and the ecosystems of which they are part."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forest_biological_biodiversity">Forest biological biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Forest biological biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Forest biological diversity is a broad term that refers to all life forms found within forested areas and the ecological roles they perform. As such, forest biological diversity encompasses not just trees, but the multitude of plants, animals and microorganisms that inhabit forest areas and their associated genetic diversity. Forest biological diversity can be considered at different levels, including ecosystem, landscape, species, population and genetic. Complex interactions can occur within and between these levels. In biologically diverse forests, this complexity allows organisms to adapt to continually changing environmental conditions and to maintain ecosystem functions.
</p><p>In the annex to Decision II/9 (CBD, n.d.a), the Conference of the Parties to the CBD recognized that: "Forest biological diversity results from evolutionary processes over thousands and even millions of years which, in themselves, are driven by ecological forces such as climate, fire, competition and disturbance. Furthermore, the diversity of forest ecosystems (in both physical and biological features) results in high levels of adaptation, a feature of forest ecosystems which is an integral component of their biological diversity. Within specific forest ecosystems, the maintenance of ecological processes is dependent upon the maintenance of their biological diversity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="549" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png/330px-Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png/495px-Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_latitudinal_gradient_of_biodiversity_mannion_2014.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of living terrestrial vertebrate species, highest concentration of diversity shown in red in equatorial regions, declining polewards (towards the blue end of the spectrum) (Mannion 2014)</div></div></div>
<p>Biodiversity is not evenly distributed, rather it varies greatly across the globe as well as within regions. Among other factors, the diversity of all living things (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a>) depends on <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a> and the presence of other species. The study of the spatial distribution of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>, species and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, is the science of <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorandKrasnov2010_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MorandKrasnov2010-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diversity consistently measures higher in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> and in other localized regions such as the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Floristic_Region" title="Cape Floristic Region">Cape Floristic Region</a> and lower in polar regions generally. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rain_forests" title="Rain forests">Rain forests</a> that have had wet climates for a long time, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yasuni_National_Park" title="Yasuni National Park">Yasuní National Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, have particularly high biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dotearth_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-dotearth-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journal.pone.0008767_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-journal.pone.0008767-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Terrestrial biodiversity is thought to be up to 25 times greater than ocean biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Forests harbour most of Earth's terrestrial biodiversity. The conservation of the world's biodiversity is thus utterly dependent on the way in which we interact with and use the world's forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-23">[23]</a></sup> A new method used in 2011, put the total number of species on Earth at 8.7 million, of which 2.1 million were estimated to live in the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-29">[29]</a></sup> However, this estimate seems to under-represent the diversity of microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-30">[30]</a></sup> Forests provide habitats for 80 percent of amphibian species, 75 percent of bird species and 68 percent of mammal species. About 60 percent of all vascular plants are found in tropical forests. Mangroves provide breeding grounds and nurseries for numerous species of fish and shellfish and help trap sediments that might otherwise adversely affect seagrass beds and coral reefs, which are habitats for many more marine species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-:8-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biodiversity of forests varies considerably according to factors such as forest type, geography, climate and soils – in addition to human use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-31">[31]</a></sup> Most forest habitats in temperate regions support relatively few animal and plant species and species that tend to have large geographical distributions, while the montane forests of Africa, South America and Southeast Asia and lowland forests of Australia, coastal Brazil, the Caribbean islands, Central America and insular Southeast Asia have many species with small geographical distributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-31">[31]</a></sup> Areas with dense human populations and intense agricultural land use, such as Europe, parts of Bangladesh, China, India and North America, are less intact in terms of their biodiversity. Northern Africa, southern Australia, coastal Brazil, Madagascar and South Africa, are also identified as areas with striking losses in biodiversity intactness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-:7-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitudinal_gradients">Latitudinal gradients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Latitudinal gradients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latitudinal_gradients_in_species_diversity" title="Latitudinal gradients in species diversity">Latitudinal gradients in species diversity</a></div>
<p>Generally, there is an increase in biodiversity from the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">poles</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>. Thus localities at lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a> have more species than localities at higher <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>. This is often referred to as the latitudinal gradient in species diversity. Several ecological factors may contribute to the gradient, but the ultimate factor behind many of them is the greater mean temperature at the equator compared to that of the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoraRobertson2003_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoraRobertson2003-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though terrestrial biodiversity declines from the equator to the poles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> some studies claim that this characteristic is unverified in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystems" title="Aquatic ecosystems">aquatic ecosystems</a>, especially in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystems" title="Marine ecosystems">marine ecosystems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The latitudinal distribution of parasites does not appear to follow this rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorandKrasnov2010_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-MorandKrasnov2010-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, an alternative hypothesis ("the <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> biodiversity") was proposed to explain the biodiversity latitudinal gradient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In this study, the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> pool size and the fractal nature of ecosystems were combined to clarify some general patterns of this gradient. This hypothesis considers <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moisture" title="Moisture">moisture</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Net_primary_production" title="Net primary production">net primary production</a> (NPP) as the main variables of an ecosystem niche and as the axis of the ecological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypervolume" title="Hypervolume">hypervolume</a>. In this way, it is possible to build fractal hyper volumes, whose <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a> rises to three moving towards the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity_Hotspot">Biodiversity Hotspot</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biodiversity Hotspot">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_hotspot" title="Biodiversity hotspot">biodiversity hotspot</a> is a region with a high level of <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> species that have experienced great <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The term hotspot was introduced in 1988 by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Myers" title="Norman Myers">Norman Myers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKee2004_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKee2004-43">[43]</a></sup> While hotspots are spread all over the world, the majority are forest areas and most are located in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Forest" title="Atlantic Forest">Atlantic Forest</a> is considered one such hotspot, containing roughly 20,000 plant species, 1,350 vertebrates and millions of insects, about half of which occur nowhere else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyersMittermeierMittermeier2000_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-MyersMittermeierMittermeier2000-45">[45]</a></sup> The island of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> are also particularly notable. <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> is characterized by high biodiversity, with the highest rate of species by area unit worldwide and it has the largest number of endemics (species that are not found naturally anywhere else) of any country. About 10% of the species of the Earth can be found in Colombia, including over 1,900 species of bird, more than in Europe and North America combined, Colombia has 10% of the world's mammals species, 14% of the amphibian species and 18% of the bird species of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Madagascar_dry_deciduous_forests" title="Madagascar dry deciduous forests">Madagascar dry deciduous forests</a> and lowland rainforests possess a high ratio of <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-48">[48]</a></sup> Since the island separated from mainland <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> 66 million years ago, many species and ecosystems have evolved independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s 17,000 islands cover 735,355 square miles (1,904,560 km<sup>2</sup>) and contain 10% of the world's <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>, 12% of mammals and 17% of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>—along with nearly 240 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Many regions of high biodiversity and/or endemism arise from specialized <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> which require unusual adaptations, for example, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_climate" title="Alpine climate">alpine</a> environments in high <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern European</a> peat <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Accurately measuring differences in biodiversity can be difficult. <a href="/wiki/Selection_bias" title="Selection bias">Selection bias</a> amongst researchers may contribute to biased empirical research for modern estimates of biodiversity. In 1768, Rev. <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a> succinctly observed of his <a href="/wiki/Selborne" title="Selborne">Selborne, Hampshire</a> <i>"all nature is so full, that that district produces the most variety which is the most examined."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White1887_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-White1887-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biodiversity is the result of 3.5 billion years of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_life" title="Origin of life">origin of life</a> has not been established by science, however, some evidence suggests that life may already have been well-established only a few hundred million years after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formation of the Earth</a>. Until approximately 2.5 billion years ago, all life consisted of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> – <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">single-celled</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protozoan" title="Protozoan">protozoans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>

none;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin:0;padding:0;top:0.000em;height:1.982em;left:2.470em;width:3.510em"><div style="font-size:100%;position:relative;top:-0.53em;left:0em"><div title="Flowering plant">Flowers</a></div></div></div><div style="font-size:100%;background-color:#e6b880;border-style:none <div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="627" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/300px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/450px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png/600px-Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phanerozoic_Biodiversity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apparent marine fossil diversity during the Phanerozoic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosing2010_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosing2010-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The history of biodiversity during the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> (the last 540 million years), starts with rapid growth during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>—a period during which nearly every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylum_(biology)" title="Phylum (biology)">phylum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> first appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-54">[54]</a></sup> Over the next 400 million years or so, <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> diversity showed little overall trend and <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> diversity shows an overall exponential trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup> This dramatic rise in diversity was marked by periodic, massive losses of diversity classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup> A significant loss occurred when rainforests collapsed in the carboniferous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010RainforestCollapse-55">[55]</a></sup> The worst was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian-Triassic extinction event">Permian-Triassic extinction event</a>, 251 million years ago. Vertebrates took 30 million years to recover from this event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biodivertisy of the past is called Paleobiodiversity. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a> suggests that the last few million years featured the greatest biodiversity in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup> However, not all scientists support this view, since there is uncertainty as to how strongly the fossil record is biased by the greater availability and preservation of recent <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geologic</a> sections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[57]</a></sup> Some scientists believe that corrected for sampling artifacts, modern biodiversity may not be much different from biodiversity 300 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-54">[54]</a></sup> whereas others consider the fossil record reasonably reflective of the diversification of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup> Estimates of the present global macroscopic species diversity vary from 2 million to 100 million, with a best estimate of somewhere near 9 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-29">[29]</a></sup> the vast majority <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heywood_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-heywood-58">[58]</a></sup> Diversity appears to increase continually in the absence of natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversification">Diversification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Diversification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The existence of a <i>global carrying capacity</i>, limiting the amount of life that can live at once, is debated, as is the question of whether such a limit would also cap the number of species. While records of life in the sea show a <a href="/wiki/Logistic_function" title="Logistic function">logistic</a> pattern of growth, life on land (insects, plants and tetrapods) shows an <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponential</a> rise in diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup> As one author states, "Tetrapods have not yet invaded 64 percent of potentially habitable modes and it could be that without human influence the ecological and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> diversity of tetrapods would continue to increase exponentially until most or all of the available eco-space is filled."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney,_S._2010_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sahney,_S._2010-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>It also appears that the diversity continues to increase over time, especially after mass extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, changes through the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> correlate much better with the <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_growth" title="Hyperbolic growth">hyperbolic</a> model (widely used in <a href="/wiki/Population_biology" title="Population biology">population biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macrosociology" title="Macrosociology">macrosociology</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> biodiversity) than with exponential and logistic models. The latter models imply that changes in diversity are guided by a first-order <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> (more ancestors, more descendants) and/or a <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">negative feedback</a> arising from resource limitation. Hyperbolic model implies a second-order positive feedback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hautmann_et_al._2015_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hautmann_et_al._2015-61">[61]</a></sup> Differences in the strength of the second-order feedback due to different intensities of interspecific competition might explain the faster rediversification of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonoids" title="Ammonoids">ammonoids</a> in comparison to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalves" title="Bivalves">bivalves</a> after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End-Permian_extinction" title="End-Permian extinction">end-Permian extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hautmann_et_al._2015_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hautmann_et_al._2015-61">[61]</a></sup> The hyperbolic pattern of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> growth arises from a second-order positive feedback between the population size and the rate of technological growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18677962_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18677962-62">[62]</a></sup> The hyperbolic character of biodiversity growth can be similarly accounted for by a feedback between diversity and community structure complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18677962_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18677962-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-63">[63]</a></sup> The similarity between the curves of biodiversity and human population probably comes from the fact that both are derived from the interference of the hyperbolic trend with cyclical and <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a> dynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18677962_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18677962-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most biologists agree however that the period since human emergence is part of a new mass extinction, named the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction_event" title="Holocene extinction event">Holocene extinction event</a>, caused primarily by the impact humans are having on the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> It has been argued that the present rate of extinction is sufficient to eliminate most species on the planet Earth within 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2002_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2002-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>New species are regularly discovered (on average between 5–10,000 new species each year, most of them <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>) and many, though discovered, are not yet classified (estimates are that nearly 90% of all <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are not yet classified).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heywood_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-heywood-58">[58]</a></sup> Most of the terrestrial diversity is found in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> and in general, the land has more species than the ocean; some 8.7 million species may exist on Earth, of which some 2.1 million live in the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moraplos_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moraplos-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_services">Ecosystem services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecosystem services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2468" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/170px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/255px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg/340px-Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field_Hamois_Belgium_Luc_Viatour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Summer field in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (Hamois). The blue flowers are <i><a href="/wiki/Centaurea_cyanus" title="Centaurea cyanus">Centaurea cyanus</a></i> and the red are <i><a href="/wiki/Papaver_rhoeas" title="Papaver rhoeas">Papaver rhoeas</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_ecosystem_services">General ecosystem services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: General ecosystem services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">Ecosystem services</a></div>
<p>"Ecosystem services are the suite of benefits that ecosystems provide to humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup> The natural species, or biota, are the caretakers of all ecosystems. It is as if the natural world is an enormous bank account of capital assets capable of paying life sustaining dividends indefinitely, but only if the capital is maintained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
These services come in three flavors:
</p>
<ol><li>Provisioning services which involve the production of renewable resources (e.g.: food, wood, fresh water)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Regulating services which are those that lessen environmental change (e.g.: climate regulation, pest/disease control)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cultural services represent human value and enjoyment (e.g.: landscape aesthetics, cultural heritage, outdoor recreation and spiritual significance)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>There have been many claims about biodiversity's effect on these ecosystem services, especially provisioning and regulating services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup> After an exhaustive survey through peer-reviewed literature to evaluate 36 different claims about biodiversity's effect on ecosystem services, 14 of those claims have been validated, 6 demonstrate mixed support or are unsupported, 3 are incorrect and 13 lack enough evidence to draw definitive conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_enhanced">Services enhanced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Services enhanced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dt>Provisioning services</dt></dl>
<p>Greater species diversity
</p>
<ul><li>of plants increases fodder yield (synthesis of 271 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants (i.e. diversity within a single species) increases overall <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yield</a> (synthesis of 575 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grain-yield-increase-in-cereal-variety-mixtures_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-grain-yield-increase-in-cereal-variety-mixtures-69">[69]</a></sup> Although another review of 100 experimental studies reports mixed evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
<li>of trees increases overall <a href="/wiki/Wood_production" title="Wood production">wood production</a> (Synthesis of 53 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tree-growth-in-monocultures-and-mixed_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-tree-growth-in-monocultures-and-mixed-71">[71]</a></sup> However, there is not enough data to draw a conclusion about the effect of tree trait diversity on wood production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-5"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Regulating services</dt></dl>
<p>Greater species diversity
</p>
<ul><li>of fish increases the stability of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> yield (Synthesis of 8 observational studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-6"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>of natural pest enemies decreases herbivorous pest populations (Data from two separate reviews; Synthesis of 266 experimental and observational studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual-review-ecology-evolution-systematics-vol-40_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-annual-review-ecology-evolution-systematics-vol-40-72">[72]</a></sup> Synthesis of 18 observational studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-functional-richness-and-ecosystem-services-bird-pred_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-functional-richness-and-ecosystem-services-bird-pred-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birds-as-predators-in-trop-agroforestry_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-birds-as-predators-in-trop-agroforestry-74">[74]</a></sup> Although another review of 38 experimental studies found mixed support for this claim, suggesting that in cases where mutual intraguild predation occurs, a single predatory species is often more effective<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-influence-of-intraguild-predation_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-influence-of-intraguild-predation-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants decreases disease prevalence on plants (Synthesis of 107 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants increases resistance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_invasion" title="Plant invasion">plant invasion</a> (Data from two separate reviews; Synthesis of 105 experimental studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-76">[76]</a></sup> Synthesis of 15 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biotic-resistance-to-exotic-plant-invasions_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-biotic-resistance-to-exotic-plant-invasions-77">[77]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>of plants increases <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a>, but note that this finding only relates to actual uptake of carbon dioxide and not long-term storage, see below; Synthesis of 479 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>plants increases <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_nutrient" title="Soil nutrient">soil nutrient</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remineralization" title="Remineralization">remineralization</a> (Synthesis of 103 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>of plants increases soil organic matter (Synthesis of 85 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-76">[76]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_with_mixed_evidence">Services with mixed evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Services with mixed evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dt>Provisioning services</dt></dl>
<ul><li>None to date</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Regulating services</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Greater species diversity of plants may or may not decrease herbivorous pest populations. Data from two separate reviews suggest that greater diversity decreases pest populations (Synthesis of 40 observational studies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-organic-agriculture-promotes-evenness-and-natural-pest-control_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-organic-agriculture-promotes-evenness-and-natural-pest-control-78">[78]</a></sup> Synthesis of 100 experimental studies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-does-plant-diversity-benefit-agroecosystems-70">[70]</a></sup> One review found mixed evidence (Synthesis of 287 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vegetational-diversity-and-arthropod-population-response_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-vegetational-diversity-and-arthropod-population-response-79">[79]</a></sup>), while another found contrary evidence (Synthesis of 100 experimental studies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-plant-diversity-enhances-provision-of-ecosystem-services-76">[76]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>Greater species diversity of animals may or may not decrease disease prevalence on those animals (Synthesis of 45 experimental and observational studies),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-impacts-of-biodiversity-on-infectious-diseases_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-impacts-of-biodiversity-on-infectious-diseases-80">[80]</a></sup> although a 2013 study offers more support showing that biodiversity may in fact enhance disease resistance within animal communities, at least in amphibian frog ponds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Many more studies must be published in support of diversity to sway the balance of evidence will be such that we can draw a general rule on this service.</li>
<li>Greater species and trait diversity of plants may or may not increase long term carbon storage (Synthesis of 33 observational studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-7"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Greater pollinator diversity may or may not increase pollination (Synthesis of 7 observational studies),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-8"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup> but a publication from March 2013 suggests that increased native pollinator diversity enhances pollen deposition (although not necessarily fruit set as the authors would have you believe, for details explore their lengthy supplementary material).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wild-pollinators-enhance-fruit-set-of-crops_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-wild-pollinators-enhance-fruit-set-of-crops-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Services_hindered">Services hindered</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Services hindered">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dt>Provisioning services</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Greater species diversity of plants reduces primary production (Synthesis of 7 experimental studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-the-functional-role-of-producer-diversity-25">[25]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Regulating services</dt></dl>
<ul><li>greater genetic and species diversity of a number of organisms reduces freshwater purification (Synthesis of 8 experimental studies, although an attempt by the authors to investigate the effect of detritivore diversity on freshwater purification was unsuccessful due to a lack of available evidence (only 1 <a href="/wiki/Observational_study" title="Observational study">observational study</a> was found<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-9"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of plants on biofuel yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 3 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-10"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of fish on fishery yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 4 experimental studies and 1 observational study)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-11"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Regulating services</dt></dl>
<ul><li>Effect of species diversity on the stability of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators did not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-12"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of plants on the stability of fodder yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-13"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of plants on the stability of crop yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 1 study)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-14"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> of plants on the stability of crop yield (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-15"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of diversity on the stability of wood production (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-16"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species diversity of multiple taxa on <a href="/wiki/Erosion_control" title="Erosion control">erosion control</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies – they did, however, find studies on the effect of species diversity and root biomass)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-17"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of diversity on <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Flood_regulation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Flood regulation (page does not exist)">flood regulation</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators could not find any studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-18"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Effect of species and trait diversity of plants on <a href="/wiki/Soil_moisture" title="Soil moisture">soil moisture</a> (In a survey of the literature, the investigators only found 2 studies)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-19"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other sources have reported somewhat conflicting results and in 1997 Robert Costanza and his colleagues reported the estimated global value of ecosystem services (not captured in traditional markets) at an average of $33 trillion annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, species loss has accelerated above the average basal rate, driven by human activity. Estimates of species losses are at a rate 100–10,000 times as fast as is typical in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan2005_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan2005-84">[84]</a></sup> Biodiversity also affords many non-material benefits including spiritual and aesthetic values, knowledge systems and education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan2005_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan2005-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_biodiversity" title="Agricultural biodiversity">Agricultural biodiversity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="964" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/250px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/375px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg/500px-Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_Manaus_forest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a></div></div></div>
<p>Agricultural diversity can be divided into two categories: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">intraspecific diversity</a>, which includes the genetic variation within a single species, like the potato (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solanum_tuberosum" title="Solanum tuberosum">Solanum tuberosum</a></i>) that is composed of many different forms and types (e.g. in the U.S. they might compare russet potatoes with new potatoes or purple potatoes, all different, but all part of the same species, <i>S. tuberosum</i>).
</p><p>The other category of agricultural diversity is called <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">interspecific diversity</a> and refers to the number and types of different species. Thinking about this diversity we might note that many small vegetable farmers grow many different crops like potatoes and also carrots, peppers, lettuce, etc.
</p><p>Agricultural diversity can also be divided by whether it is 'planned' diversity or 'associated' diversity. This is a functional classification that we impose and not an intrinsic feature of life or diversity. Planned diversity includes the crops which a farmer has encouraged, planted or raised (e.g. crops, covers, symbionts, and livestock, among others), which can be contrasted with the associated diversity that arrives among the crops, uninvited (e.g. herbivores, weed species and pathogens, among others).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecology-of-agroecosystems_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecology-of-agroecosystems-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Associated biodiversity can be damaging or beneficial. The beneficial associated biodiversity include for instance wild pollinators such as wild bees and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrphid" title="Syrphid">syrphid</a> flies that pollinate crops<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPBES_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPBES-86">[86]</a></sup> and natural enemies and antagonists to pests and pathogens. Beneficial associated biodiversity occurs abundantly in crop fields and provide multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> such as pest control, nutrient cycling and pollination that support crop production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The control of damaging associated biodiversity is one of the great agricultural challenges that farmers face. On <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> farms, the approach is generally to suppress damaging associated diversity using a suite of biologically destructive <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, mechanized tools and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">transgenic engineering techniques</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">rotate crops</a>. Although some <a href="/wiki/Polyculture" title="Polyculture">polyculture</a> farmers use the same techniques, they also employ <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a> strategies as well as more labor-intensive strategies, but generally less dependent on capital, biotechnology, and energy.
</p><p>Interspecific crop diversity is, in part, responsible for offering variety in what we eat. Intraspecific diversity, the variety of alleles within a single species, also offers us a choice in our diets. If a crop fails in a monoculture, we rely on agricultural diversity to replant the land with something new. If a wheat crop is destroyed by a pest we may plant a hardier variety of wheat the next year, relying on intraspecific diversity. We may forgo wheat production in that area and plant a different species altogether, relying on interspecific diversity. Even an agricultural society that primarily grows monocultures relies on biodiversity at some point.
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish potato blight</a> of 1846 was a major factor in the deaths of one million people and the emigration of about two million. It was the result of planting only two potato varieties, both vulnerable to the blight, <i><a href="/wiki/Phytophthora_infestans" title="Phytophthora infestans">Phytophthora infestans</a></i>, which arrived in 1845<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecology-of-agroecosystems_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-ecology-of-agroecosystems-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>When <a href="/wiki/Rice_grassy_stunt_virus" title="Rice grassy stunt virus">rice grassy stunt virus</a> struck rice fields from Indonesia to India in the 1970s, 6,273 varieties were tested for resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-88">[88]</a></sup> Only one was resistant, an Indian variety and known to science only since 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-88">[88]</a></sup> This variety formed a hybrid with other varieties and is now widely grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RGSV_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-RGSV-88">[88]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coffee_rust" title="Coffee rust">Coffee rust</a> attacked coffee plantations in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and Central America in 1970. A resistant variety was found in Ethiopia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The diseases are themselves a form of biodiversity.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">Monoculture</a> was a contributing factor to several agricultural disasters, including the European wine industry collapse in the late 19th century and the US <a href="/wiki/Southern_corn_leaf_blight" title="Southern corn leaf blight">southern corn leaf blight</a> epidemic of 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although about 80 percent of humans' food supply comes from just 20 kinds of plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> humans use at least 40,000 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Earth's surviving biodiversity provides resources for increasing the range of food and other products suitable for human use, although the present extinction rate shrinks that potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2002_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2002-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_health">Human health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Human health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/170px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/255px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png/340px-Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forest_fruits_from_Barro_Colorado.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The diverse forest canopy on <a href="/wiki/Barro_Colorado_Island" title="Barro Colorado Island">Barro Colorado Island</a>, Panama, yielded this display of different fruit</div></div></div>
<p>Biodiversity's relevance to human health is becoming an international political issue, as scientific evidence builds on the global health implications of biodiversity loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiversity1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-biodiversity1-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CorvalánHales2005_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-CorvalánHales2005-95">[95]</a></sup> This issue is closely linked with the issue of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> as many of the anticipated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Health_risks_of_climate_change" title="Health risks of climate change">health risks of climate change</a> are associated with changes in biodiversity (e.g. changes in populations and distribution of disease vectors, scarcity of fresh water, impacts on agricultural biodiversity and food resources etc.). This is because the species most likely to disappear are those that buffer against infectious disease transmission, while surviving species tend to be the ones that increase disease transmission, such as that of West Nile Virus, <a href="/wiki/Lyme_disease" title="Lyme disease">Lyme disease</a> and Hantavirus, according to a study done co-authored by Felicia Keesing, an ecologist at Bard College and Drew Harvell, associate director for Environment of the <a href="/wiki/Atkinson_Center_for_a_Sustainable_Future" title="Atkinson Center for a Sustainable Future">Atkinson Center for a Sustainable Future</a> (ACSF) at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The growing demand and lack of drinkable water on the planet presents an additional challenge to the future of human health. Partly, the problem lies in the success of water suppliers to increase supplies and failure of groups promoting the preservation of water resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bank2010_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bank2010-98">[98]</a></sup> While the distribution of clean water increases, in some parts of the world it remains unequal. According to the World Health Organisation (2018), only 71% of the global population used a safely managed drinking-water service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the health issues influenced by biodiversity include dietary health and nutrition security, infectious disease, medical science and medicinal resources, social and psychological health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Biodiversity is also known to have an important role in reducing disaster risk and in post-disaster relief and recovery efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
