</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> a <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogen</a>, like a <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a>, have more chances to meet resistance in a diverse population. Therefore, in a population genetically similar it expands more easily. For example, the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> had less chances to occur in a world with higher biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biodiversity provides critical support for drug discovery and the availability of medicinal resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> A significant proportion of drugs are derived, directly or indirectly, from biological sources: at least 50% of the pharmaceutical compounds on the US market are derived from plants, animals and <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, while about 80% of the world population depends on medicines from nature (used in either modern or traditional medical practice) for primary healthcare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biodiversity1_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-biodiversity1-94">[94]</a></sup> Only a tiny fraction of wild species has been investigated for medical potential. Biodiversity has been critical to advances throughout the field of <a href="/wiki/Bionics" title="Bionics">bionics</a>. Evidence from market analysis and biodiversity science indicates that the decline in output from the pharmaceutical sector since the mid-1980s can be attributed to a move away from natural product exploration ("bioprospecting") in favour of genomics and synthetic chemistry, indeed claims about the value of undiscovered pharmaceuticals may not provide enough incentive for companies in free markets to search for them because of the high cost of development;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> meanwhile, natural products have a long history of supporting significant economic and health innovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Marine ecosystems are particularly important,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> although inappropriate <a href="/wiki/Bioprospecting" title="Bioprospecting">bioprospecting</a> can increase biodiversity loss, as well as violating the laws of the communities and states from which the resources are taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_and_industry">Business and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Business and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ueberladewagen_(jha).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="1094" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/220px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/330px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg/440px-Ueberladewagen_%28jha%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ueberladewagen_(jha).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> production, pictured is a <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">tractor</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Chaser_bin" title="Chaser bin">chaser bin</a></div></div></div>
<p>Many industrial materials derive directly from biological sources. These include building materials, fibers, dyes, rubber, and oil. Biodiversity is also important to the security of resources such as water, timber, paper, fiber, and food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> As a result, biodiversity loss is a significant risk factor in business development and a threat to long-term economic sustainability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Leisure.2C_cultural_and_aesthetic_value"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leisure,_cultural_and_aesthetic_value">Leisure, cultural and aesthetic value</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Leisure, cultural and aesthetic value">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biodiversity enriches leisure activities such as <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatching</a> or natural history study.
</p><p>Popular activities such as <a href="/wiki/Gardening" title="Gardening">gardening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fishkeeping" title="Fishkeeping">fishkeeping</a> strongly depend on biodiversity. The number of species involved in such pursuits is in the tens of thousands, though the majority do not enter commerce.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The relationships between the original natural areas of these often exotic animals and plants and commercial collectors, suppliers, breeders, propagators and those who promote their understanding and enjoyment are complex and poorly understood. The general public responds well to exposure to rare and unusual organisms, reflecting their inherent value.
</p><p>Philosophically it could be argued that biodiversity has intrinsic aesthetic and spiritual value to <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">mankind</a> <i>in and of itself</i>. This idea can be used as a counterweight to the notion that <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> and other ecological realms are only worthy of conservation because of the services they provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="533" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg/170px-Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg/255px-Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg/340px-Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eaglecreek-28July2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eagle_Creek_(Multnomah_County,_Oregon)" title="Eagle Creek (Multnomah County, Oregon)">Eagle Creek</a>, Oregon hiking</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_services">Ecological services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ecological services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ecological_effects_of_biodiversity" title="Ecological effects of biodiversity">Ecological effects of biodiversity</a></div>
<p>Biodiversity supports many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"There is now unequivocal evidence that biodiversity loss reduces the efficiency by which ecological communities capture biologically essential resources, produce biomass, decompose and recycle biologically essential nutrients... There is mounting evidence that biodiversity increases the stability of ecosystem functions through time... Diverse communities are more productive because they contain key species that have a large influence on productivity and differences in functional traits among organisms increase total resource capture... The impacts of diversity loss on ecological processes might be sufficiently large to rival the impacts of many other global drivers of environmental change... Maintaining multiple ecosystem processes at multiple places and times requires higher levels of biodiversity than does a single process at a single place and time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diversity-loss-and-its-impact_66-20"><a href="#cite_note-diversity-loss-and-its-impact-66">[66]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>It plays a part in regulating the chemistry of our <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a>. Biodiversity is directly involved in <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">water purification</a>, recycling <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> and providing fertile soils. Experiments with controlled environments have shown that humans cannot easily build ecosystems to support human needs;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> for example <a href="/wiki/Entomophily" title="Entomophily">insect pollination</a> cannot be mimicked, though there have been attempts to create artificial pollinators using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicles" title="Unmanned aerial vehicles">unmanned aerial vehicles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The economic activity of pollination alone represented between $2.1–14.6 billion in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_species">Number of species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Number of species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_biodiversity" title="Global biodiversity">Global biodiversity</a></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1958" data-file-width="4103" decoding="async" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png/700px-Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png/1050px-Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png/1400px-Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png 2x" width="700"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mora_2011_Predicted_and_Unpredicted_species.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Discovered and predicted total number of species on land and in the oceans</div></div></div></div>
<p>According to Mora and colleagues, the total number of terrestrial species is estimated to be around 8.7 million while the number of oceanic species is much lower, estimated at 2.2 million. The authors note that these estimates are strongest for eukaryotic organisms and likely represent the lower bound of prokaryote diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mora2011_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-mora2011-122">[122]</a></sup> Other estimates include:
</p>
<ul><li>220,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_plants" title="Vascular plants">vascular plants</a>, estimated using the species-area relation method<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></li>
<li>0.7-1 million marine species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li>10–30 million <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> (of some 0.9 million we know today)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Le_Monde_newspaper_article_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Le_Monde_newspaper_article-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li>5–10 million <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>1.5-3 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, estimates based on data from the tropics, long-term non-tropical sites and molecular studies that have revealed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptic_species" title="Cryptic species">cryptic speciation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Some 0.075 million species of fungi had been documented by 2001;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawksworth_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawksworth-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>1 million <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></li>
<li>The number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbial" title="Microbial">microbial</a> species is not reliably known, but the <a href="/wiki/Global_Ocean_Sampling_Expedition" title="Global Ocean Sampling Expedition">Global Ocean Sampling Expedition</a> dramatically increased the estimates of genetic diversity by identifying an enormous number of new genes from near-surface <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> samples at various marine locations, initially over the 2004–2006 period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The findings may eventually cause a significant change in the way science defines <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and other taxonomic categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Since the rate of extinction has increased, many extant species may become extinct before they are described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Not surprisingly, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animalia" title="Animalia">animalia</a> the most studied groups are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishes" title="Fishes">fishes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropods" title="Arthropods">arthropods</a> are the least studied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">animals</a> groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring_biodiversity">Measuring biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Measuring biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Diversity_index" title="Diversity index">diversity index</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measurement_of_biodiversity" title="Measurement of biodiversity">measurement of biodiversity</a></div>
<p>A variety of objective means exist to empirically measure biodiversity. Each measure relates to a particular use of the data, and is likely to be associated with the variety of genes. Biodiversity is commonly measured in terms of taxonomic richness of a geographic area over a time interval.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2019</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_loss_rates">Species loss rates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Species loss rates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_biodiversity" title="Loss of biodiversity">Loss of biodiversity</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No longer do we have to justify the existence of humid tropical forests on the feeble grounds that they might carry plants with drugs that cure human disease. <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis"><i>Gaia theory</i></a> forces us to see that they offer much more than this. Through their capacity to evapotranspirate vast volumes of water vapor, they serve to keep the planet cool by wearing a sunshade of white reflecting cloud. Their replacement by cropland could precipitate a disaster that is global in scale.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a>, in <i>Biodiversity</i> (<a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> (Ed))<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeakeyLewin1996_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeakeyLewin1996-136">[136]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>During the last century, decreases in biodiversity have been increasingly observed. In 2007, German Federal Environment Minister <a href="/wiki/Sigmar_Gabriel" title="Sigmar Gabriel">Sigmar Gabriel</a> cited estimates that up to 30% of all species will be extinct by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Of these, about one eighth of known plant species are threatened with <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reid_Reversing_loss_of_Biodiversity_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reid_Reversing_loss_of_Biodiversity-138">[138]</a></sup> Estimates reach as high as 140,000 species per year (based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species-area_curve" title="Species-area curve">Species-area theory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> This figure indicates <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">unsustainable</a> ecological practices, because few species emerge each year.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Almost all scientists acknowledge that the rate of species loss is greater now than at any time in human history, with extinctions occurring at rates hundreds of times higher than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Background_extinction" title="Background extinction">background extinction</a> rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reid_Reversing_loss_of_Biodiversity_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reid_Reversing_loss_of_Biodiversity-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dasgupta_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dasgupta-141">[141]</a></sup> and expected to still grow in the upcoming years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dasgupta_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dasgupta-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> As of 2012, some studies suggest that 25% of all mammal species could be extinct in 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>In absolute terms, the planet has lost 58% of its biodiversity since 1970 according to a 2016 study by the World Wildlife Fund.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The Living Planet Report 2014 claims that "the number of mammals, birds, reptiles, amphibians, and fish across the globe is, on average, about half the size it was 40 years ago". Of that number, 39% accounts for the terrestrial wildlife gone, 39% for the marine wildlife gone and 76% for the freshwater wildlife gone. Biodiversity took the biggest hit in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, plummeting 83 percent. High-income countries showed a 10% increase in biodiversity, which was canceled out by a loss in low-income countries. This is despite the fact that high-income countries use five times the ecological resources of low-income countries, which was explained as a result of a process whereby wealthy nations are outsourcing <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource depletion</a> to poorer nations, which are suffering the greatest ecosystem losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivingPlanetReport2014_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-LivingPlanetReport2014-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/PLOS_One" title="PLOS One">PLOS One</a></i> found that the biomass of insect life in Germany had declined by three-quarters in the last 25 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Dave Goulson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sussex_University" title="Sussex University">Sussex University</a> stated that their study suggested that humans "appear to be making vast tracts of land inhospitable to most forms of life, and are currently on course for ecological Armageddon. If we lose the insects then everything is going to collapse."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Foundation" title="World Wildlife Foundation">World Wildlife Foundation</a> published a report saying that "biodiversity is being destroyed at a rate unprecedented in human history". The report claims that 68% of the population of the examined species were destroyed in the years 1970 – 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats">Threats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Threats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flii_globe.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="832" data-file-width="1657" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Flii_globe.png/300px-Flii_globe.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Flii_globe.png/450px-Flii_globe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Flii_globe.png/600px-Flii_globe.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flii_globe.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> measures global anthropogenic modification on remaining forests annually. 0 = Most modification; 10= Least.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flii_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-flii-150">[150]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, many species were formally classified as <a href="/wiki/Rare_species" title="Rare species">rare</a> or <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered</a> or <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">threatened</a>; moreover, scientists have estimated that millions more species are at risk which have not been formally recognized. About 40 percent of the 40,177 species assessed using the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> criteria are now listed as threatened with <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>—a total of 16,119.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The five main drivers to biodiversity loss are : habitat loss, invasive species, overexploitation (extreme hunting and fishing pressure), pollution, and climate change.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> describes an "Evil Quartet" of <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overhunting" title="Overhunting">overkill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> and secondary extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoultonSanderson1998_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoultonSanderson1998-152">[152]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_O._Wilson" title="Edward O. Wilson">Edward O. Wilson</a> prefers the <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> HIPPO, standing for <b>H</b>abitat destruction, <b>I</b>nvasive species, <b>P</b>ollution, <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">human over-<b>P</b>opulation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overharvesting" title="Overharvesting"><b>O</b>ver-harvesting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen2003_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen2003-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> the main direct threats to conservation fall in 11 categories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>1.  Residential &amp; commercial development</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li>housing &amp; <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban areas</a><i> (urban areas, suburbs, villages, vacation homes, shopping areas, offices, schools, hospitals)</i></li>
<li>commercial &amp; industrial areas<i> (manufacturing plants, shopping centers, office parks, military bases, power plants, train &amp; shipyards, airports)</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_tourism" title="Environmental impact of tourism">tourism</a> &amp; recreational areas<i> (skiing, golf courses, sports fields, parks, campgrounds)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>2.  Farming activities</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_agriculture" title="Environmental impact of agriculture">agriculture</a><i> (crop farms, orchards, vineyards, plantations, ranches)</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> <i>(shrimp or finfish aquaculture, fish ponds on farms, hatchery salmon, seeded shellfish beds, artificial algal beds)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>3. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_production" title="Energy production">Energy production</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a></b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> production <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal" title="Geothermal">geothermal</a>, solar, wind, &amp; tidal farms)</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-renewable_energy" title="Non-renewable energy">non-renewable energy</a> production <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_and_gas_drilling" title="Oil and gas drilling">oil and gas drilling</a>)</i></li>
<li>mining <i>(fuel and minerals)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>4.  Transportation &amp; service corridors</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li>service corridors <i>(electrical &amp; phone wires, aqueducts, oil &amp; gas pipelines)</i></li>
<li>transport corridors <i>(roads, railroads, shipping lanes, and flight paths)</i></li>
<li>collisions with the vehicles using the corridors</li>
<li>associated accidents and catastrophes <i>(<a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>, electrocution, fire)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>5.  Biological resource usages</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> <i>(bushmeat, trophy, fur)</i></li>
<li>persecution <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_control" title="Predator control">predator control</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a>, superstitions)</i></li>
<li>plant destruction or removal <i>(human consumption, free-range livestock foraging, battling timber disease, orchid collection)</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a> or wood harvesting <i>(selective or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clear-cutting" title="Clear-cutting">clear-cutting</a>, firewood collection, charcoal production)</i></li>
<li>fishing <i>(trawling, whaling, live coral or seaweed or egg collection)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>6.  Human intrusions &amp; activities that alter, destroy, simply disturb habitats and species from exhibiting natural behaviors</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li>recreational activities <i>(off-road vehicles, motorboats, jet-skis, snowmobiles, ultralight planes, dive boats, whale watching, mountain bikes, hikers, birdwatchers, skiers, pets in recreational areas, temporary campsites, caving, rock-climbing)</i></li>
<li>war, civil unrest, &amp; military exercises <i>(armed conflict, minefields, tanks &amp; other military vehicles, training exercises &amp; ranges, defoliation, munitions testing)</i></li>
<li>illegal activities <i>(<a href="/wiki/Smuggling" title="Smuggling">smuggling</a>, immigration, vandalism)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>7.  Natural system modifications</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li>fire suppression or creation <i>(controlled burns, inappropriate fire management, escaped agricultural and <a href="/wiki/Campfire" title="Campfire">campfires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">arson</a>)</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_management" title="Water management">water management</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dam</a> construction &amp; operation, <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> filling, surface water diversion, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Groundwater_exploitation" title="Groundwater exploitation">groundwater pumping</a>)</i></li>
<li>other modifications <i>(<a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">land reclamation</a> projects, shoreline <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rip-rap" title="Rip-rap">rip-rap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawn" title="Lawn">lawn</a> cultivation, beach construction and maintenance, tree-thinning in parks)</i></li>
<li>removing/reducing human maintenance <i>(mowing meadows, reduction in controlled burns, lack of indigenous management of key ecosystems, ceasing supplemental feeding of condors)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>8.  Invasive &amp; problematic species, pathogens &amp; genes</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> <i>(feral horses &amp; household pets, zebra mussels, Miconia tree, kudzu, introduction for biocontrol)</i></li>
<li>problematic native species <i>(overabundant native deer or kangaroo, overabundant algae due to loss of native grazing fish, locust-type plagues)</i></li>
<li>introduced genetic material <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticide-resistant" title="Pesticide-resistant">pesticide-resistant</a> crops, genetically modified insects for biocontrol, genetically modified trees or salmon, escaped hatchery salmon, restoration projects using non-local seed stock)</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> &amp; microbes <i>(plague affecting rodents or rabbits, Dutch elm disease or chestnut blight, Chytrid fungus affecting amphibians outside of Africa)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>9. <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">Pollution</a></b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> <i>(untreated sewage, discharges from poorly functioning <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Septic_tank" title="Septic tank">septic tanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pit_latrine" title="Pit latrine">pit latrines</a>, oil or sediment from roads, fertilizers and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> from lawns and golf courses, road salt)</i></li>
<li>industrial &amp; military effluents <i>(toxic chemicals from factories, illegal dumping of chemicals, mine tailings, arsenic from gold mining, leakage from fuel tanks, PCBs in river sediments)</i></li>
<li>agricultural &amp; forestry effluents <i>(nutrient loading from fertilizer run-off, herbicide run-off, manure from feedlots, nutrients from aquaculture, soil erosion)</i></li>
<li>garbage &amp; solid waste <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipal_waste" title="Municipal waste">municipal waste</a>, litter &amp; dumped possessions, flotsam &amp; jetsam from recreational boats, waste that entangles wildlife, <a href="/wiki/Construction_waste" title="Construction waste">construction debris</a>)</i></li>
<li>air-borne pollutants <i>(<a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vehicle_emissions" title="Vehicle emissions">vehicle emissions</a>, excess nitrogen deposition, radioactive fallout, wind dispersion of pollutants or sediments from farm fields, smoke from forest fires or wood stoves)</i></li>
<li>excess energy <i>(<a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">noise</a> from highways or airplanes, sonar from submarines that disturbs whales, heated water from power plants, lamps attracting insects, beach lights disorienting turtles, atmospheric radiation from ozone holes)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>10. Catastrophic geological events</b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>, avalanches, <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, &amp; volcanic eruptions and gas emissions</li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><b>11. <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate changes</a></b>
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li>ecosystem encroachment <i>(inundation of shoreline ecosystems &amp; drowning of coral reefs from sea level rise, dune encroachment from desertification, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bush_encroachment" title="Bush encroachment">woody encroachment</a> into grasslands)</i></li>
<li>changes in geochemical regimes <i>(<a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>, changes in atmospheric CO2 affecting plant growth, loss of sediment leading to broad-scale subsidence)</i></li>
<li>changes in temperature regimes <i>(<a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">heat waves</a>, cold spells, oceanic temperature changes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melting_of_glaciers" title="Melting of glaciers">melting of glaciers</a>/sea ice)</i></li>
<li>changes in precipitation &amp; hydrological regimes <i>(<a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">droughts</a>, rain timing, loss of snow cover, increased severity of floods)</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a> events <i>(thunderstorms, tropical storms, hurricanes, cyclones, tornadoes, hailstorms, ice storms or blizzards, dust storms, erosion of beaches during storms)</i></li></ul></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_destruction">Habitat destruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Habitat destruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sugarcane_Deforestation,_Bolivia,_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3953" data-file-width="5270" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg/220px-Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg/330px-Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg/440px-Sugarcane_Deforestation%2C_Bolivia%2C_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sugarcane_Deforestation,_Bolivia,_2016-06-15_by_Planet_Labs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> and increased road-building in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">Amazon Rainforest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> cause significant concern because of increased human encroachment upon wild areas, increased resource extraction and further threats to biodiversity.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">Habitat destruction</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">Habitat</a> destruction has played a key role in extinctions, especially in relation to <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" title="Tropical forest">tropical forest</a> destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EhrlichEhrlich1983_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-EhrlichEhrlich1983-156">[156]</a></sup> Factors contributing to habitat loss include: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">overconsumption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_use,_land-use_change_and_forestry" title="Land use, land-use change and forestry">land use change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> (<a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> or climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>Habitat size and numbers of species are systematically related. Physically larger species and those living at lower latitudes or in forests or oceans are more sensitive to reduction in habitat area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Conversion to "trivial" standardized ecosystems (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a> following <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>) effectively destroys habitat for the more diverse species that preceded the conversion. Even the simplest forms of agriculture affect diversity – through clearing/draining the land, discouraging weeds and "pests", and encouraging just a limited set of domesticated plant and animal species. In some countries, property rights<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> or lax law/regulatory enforcement are associated with deforestation and habitat loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2007 study conducted by the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> found that biodiversity and genetic diversity are codependent—that diversity among species requires diversity within a species and <i>vice versa</i>. "If anyone type is removed from the system, the cycle can break down and the community becomes dominated by a single species."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enn_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-enn-163">[163]</a></sup>
At present<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the most threatened ecosystems occur in <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a>, according to the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Ecosystem_Assessment" title="Millennium Ecosystem Assessment">Millennium Ecosystem Assessment</a> 2005, which was confirmed by the "Freshwater Animal Diversity Assessment" organised by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GBIF" title="GBIF">biodiversity platform</a> and the French <i><a href="/wiki/Institut_de_recherche_pour_le_d%C3%A9veloppement" title="Institut de recherche pour le développement">Institut de recherche pour le développement</a></i> (MNHNP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Co-extinctions are a form of <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>. Co-extinction occurs when the extinction or decline in one species accompanies similar processes in another, such as in plants and beetles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2019 report has revealed that bees and other pollinating insects have been wiped out of almost a quarter of their habitats across the United Kingdom. The population crashes have been happening since the 1980s and are affecting biodiversity. The increase in industrial farming and pesticide use, combined with diseases, invasive species, and climate change is threatening the future of these insects and the agriculture they support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, research was published showing that <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> are destroyed by human activities like <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_poisoning" title="Pesticide poisoning">pesticide poisoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a> at a rate that will cause the collapse of ecological systems in the next 50 years if it cannot be stopped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduced_and_invasive_species">Introduced and invasive species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Introduced and invasive species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">Introduced species</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">Invasive species</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg/220px-Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg/330px-Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Male_Silver_Pheasant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Male <i>Lophura nycthemera</i> (<a href="/wiki/Silver_pheasant" title="Silver pheasant">silver pheasant</a>), a native of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> that has been introduced into parts of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> for ornamental reasons</div></div></div>
<p>Barriers such as large <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">seas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceans" title="Oceans">oceans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountains" title="Mountains">mountains</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deserts" title="Deserts">deserts</a> encourage diversity by enabling independent evolution on either side of the barrier, via the process of <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric speciation</a>. The term <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> is applied to species that breach the natural barriers that would normally keep them constrained. Without barriers, such species occupy new territory, often supplanting native species by occupying their niches, or by using resources that would normally sustain native species.
</p><p>The number of species invasions has been on the rise at least since the beginning of the 1900s. Species are increasingly being moved by humans (on purpose and accidentally). In some cases the invaders are causing drastic changes and damage to their new habitats (e.g.: zebra mussels and the emerald ash borer in the Great Lakes region and the lion fish along the North American Atlantic coast). Some evidence suggests that invasive species are competitive in their new habitats because they are subject to less pathogen disturbance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Others report confounding evidence that occasionally suggest that species-rich communities harbor many native and exotic species simultaneously<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> while some say that diverse ecosystems are more resilient and resist invasive plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> An important question is, "do invasive species cause extinctions?" Many studies cite effects of invasive species on natives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> but not extinctions. Invasive species seem to increase local (i.e.: <a href="/wiki/Alpha_diversity" title="Alpha diversity">alpha diversity</a>) diversity, which decreases turnover of diversity (i.e.: <a href="/wiki/Beta_diversity" title="Beta diversity">beta diversity</a>). Overall <a href="/wiki/Gamma_diversity" title="Gamma diversity">gamma diversity</a> may be lowered because species are going extinct because of other causes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> but even some of the most insidious invaders (e.g.: Dutch elm disease, emerald ash borer, chestnut blight in North America) have not caused their host species to become extinct. <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">Extirpation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Species_homogeneity" title="Species homogeneity">homogenization</a> of regional biodiversity are much more common. Human activities have frequently been the cause of invasive species circumventing their barriers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> by introducing them for food and other purposes. Human activities therefore allow species to migrate to new areas (and thus become invasive) occurred on time scales much shorter than historically have been required for a species to extend its range.
</p><p>Not all introduced species are invasive, nor all invasive species deliberately introduced. In cases such as the <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">zebra mussel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasion</a> of US waterways was unintentional. In other cases, such as <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongooses</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, the introduction is deliberate but ineffective (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a> <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a> were not vulnerable to the <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> mongoose). In other cases, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_palm" title="Oil palm">oil palms</a> in Indonesia and Malaysia, the introduction produces substantial economic benefits, but the benefits are accompanied by costly <a href="/wiki/Unintended_consequences" title="Unintended consequences">unintended consequences</a>.
</p><p>Finally, an introduced species may unintentionally injure a species that depends on the species it replaces. In <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Prunus_spinosa" title="Prunus spinosa">Prunus spinosa</a></i> from Eastern Europe leafs much sooner than its West European counterparts, disrupting the feeding habits of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thecla_betulae" title="Thecla betulae">Thecla betulae</a></i> butterfly (which feeds on the leaves). Introducing new species often leaves endemic and other local species unable to compete with the exotic species and unable to survive. The exotic organisms may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, or may simply outcompete indigenous species for nutrients, water and light.
</p><p>At present, several countries have already imported so many exotic species, particularly agricultural and ornamental plants, that their indigenous fauna/flora may be outnumbered. For example, the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">kudzu</a> from Southeast Asia to Canada and the United States has threatened biodiversity in certain areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Nature offers effective ways to help mitigate climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_pollution">Genetic pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Genetic pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">Genetic pollution</a></div>
<p>Endemic species can be threatened with <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> through the process of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">genetic pollution</a>, i.e. uncontrolled <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">introgression</a> and genetic swamping. Genetic pollution leads to homogenization or replacement of local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genomes" title="Genomes">genomes</a> as a result of either a numerical and/or <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> advantage of an introduced species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
Hybridization and introgression are side-effects of introduction and invasion. These phenomena can be especially detrimental to <a href="/wiki/Rare_species" title="Rare species">rare species</a> that come into contact with more abundant ones. The abundant species can interbreed with the rare species, swamping its <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a>. This problem is not always apparent from <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> (outward appearance) observations alone. Some degree of <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> is normal adaptation and not all <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> constellations can be preserved. However, hybridization with or without introgression may, nevertheless, threaten a rare species' existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PottsBarbour2001_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-PottsBarbour2001-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overexploitation">Overexploitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Overexploitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">Overexploitation</a></div>
<p>Overexploitation occurs when a resource is consumed at an unsustainable rate. This occurs on land in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overhunting" title="Overhunting">overhunting</a>, excessive <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a>, poor <a href="/wiki/Soil_conservation" title="Soil conservation">soil conservation</a> in agriculture and the illegal <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_trade" title="Wildlife trade">wildlife trade</a>. Overexploitation can lead to resource destruction, including extinction.  Artificially developed projects can cause damage to the surrounding environment
</p><p>About 25% of world <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a> are now overfished to the point where their current biomass is less than the level that maximizes their sustainable yield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overkill_hypothesis" title="Overkill hypothesis">overkill hypothesis</a>, a pattern of large animal extinctions connected with <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">human migration</a> patterns, can be used to explain why <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafaunal</a> extinctions can occur within a relatively short time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hybridization.2C_genetic_pollution.2Ferosion_and_food_security"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybridization,_genetic_pollution/erosion_and_food_security">Hybridization, genetic pollution/erosion and food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hybridization, genetic pollution/erosion and food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="252" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg/220px-Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Yecoro <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> (right) <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> is sensitive to salinity, plants resulting from a hybrid cross with cultivar W4910 (left) show greater tolerance to high salinity</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_erosion" title="Genetic erosion">Genetic erosion</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> popularized the use of conventional <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> to increase yield. Often hybridized breeds originated in developed countries and were further hybridized with local varieties in the developing world to create high yield strains resistant to local climate and diseases. Local governments and industry have been pushing hybridization. Formerly huge gene pools of various wild and indigenous breeds have collapsed causing widespread <a href="/wiki/Genetic_erosion" title="Genetic erosion">genetic erosion</a> and genetic pollution. This has resulted in the loss of genetic diversity and biodiversity as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulletin28_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-bulletin28-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organisms</a> contain genetic material that is altered through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a> have become a common source for genetic pollution in not only wild varieties, but also in domesticated varieties derived from classical hybridization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zaid1999_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zaid1999-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genetic erosion and genetic pollution have the potential to destroy unique <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotypes</a>, threatening future access to <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>. A decrease in genetic diversity weakens the ability of crops and livestock to be hybridized to resist disease and survive changes in climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulletin28_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-bulletin28-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effect_of_climate_change_on_plant_biodiversity" title="Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity">Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_ecosystems" title="Effects of climate change on ecosystems">Effects of climate change on ecosystems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/220px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/330px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/440px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">Polar bears</a> on the sea ice of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>. Climate change has started affecting bear populations.</div></div></div>
<p>Global warming is a major threat to global biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kannan2009_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kannan2009-189">[189]</a></sup> For example, coral reefs – which are biodiversity hotspots – will be lost within the century if global warming continues at the current rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change has proven to affect biodiversity and evidence supporting the altering effects is widespread. Increasing atmospheric carbon dioxide certainly affects plant morphology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> and is acidifying oceans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and temperature affects species ranges,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LovejoyHannah2005_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-LovejoyHannah2005-196">[196]</a></sup> phenology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> and weather,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> but, mercifully, the major impacts that have been predicted are still potential futures. We have not documented major extinctions yet, even as climate change drastically alters the biology of many species.
</p><p>In 2004, an international collaborative study on four continents estimated that 10 percent of species would become extinct by 2050 because of global warming. "We need to limit climate change or we wind up with a lot of species in trouble, possibly extinct," said Dr. Lee Hannah, a co-author of the paper and chief climate change biologist at the Center for Applied Biodiversity Science at Conservation International.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>A recent study predicts that up to 35% of the world terrestrial carnivores and ungulates will be at higher risk of extinction by 2050 because of the joint effects of predicted climate and land-use change under business-as-usual human development scenarios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change has advanced the time of evening when Brazilian free-tailed bats (<i>Tadarida brasiliensis</i>) emerge to feed.  This change is believed to be related to the drying of regions as temperatures rise. This earlier emergence exposes the bats to greater predation increased competition with other insectivores who feed in the twilight or daylight hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_overpopulation">Human overpopulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Human overpopulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">Human overpopulation</a></div>
<p>The world's population numbered nearly 7.6 billion as of mid-2017 (which is approximately one billion more inhabitants compared to 2005) and is forecast to reach 11.1 billion in 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_King_(scientist)" title="David King (scientist)">David King</a>, former chief scientific adviser to the UK government, told a parliamentary inquiry: "It is self-evident that the massive growth in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_population" title="Human population">human population</a> through the 20th century has had more impact on biodiversity than any other single factor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> At least until the middle of the 21st century, worldwide losses of pristine biodiverse land will probably depend much on the worldwide <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">human birth rate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some top scientists have argued that population size and growth, along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">overconsumption</a>, are significant factors in <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a> and soil degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> The 2019 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPBES" title="IPBES">IPBES</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Global_Assessment_Report_on_Biodiversity_and_Ecosystem_Services" title="Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services">Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services</a></i> and biologists including <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Pimm" title="Stuart Pimm">Stuart Pimm</a> have noted that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_population_growth" title="Human population growth">human population growth</a> and overconsumption are the main drivers of species decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a>, who contends that human population growth has been devastating to the planet's biodiversity, stated that the "pattern of human population growth in the 20th century was more bacterial than primate."  He added that when <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> reached a population of six billion their <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> exceeded that of any other large land dwelling animal species that had ever existed by over 100 times, and that "we and the rest of life cannot afford another 100 years like that."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2020 study by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a>, the global human population already exceeds planet's <a href="/wiki/Biocapacity" title="Biocapacity">biocapacity</a> – it would take the equivalent of 1.56 Earths of biocapacity to meet our current demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The 2014 report further points that if everyone on the planet had the Footprint of the average resident of Qatar, we would need 4.8 Earths and if we lived the lifestyle of a typical resident of the US, we would need 3.9 Earths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivingPlanetReport2014_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-LivingPlanetReport2014-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Holocene_extinction">The Holocene extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biodiversity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The Holocene extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_(red)_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="725" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg/220px-Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg/330px-Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg/440px-Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_%28red%29_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Summary_of_major_environmental-change_categories_expressed_as_a_percentage_change_(red)_relative_to_baseline_-_fcosc-01-615419-g001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Summary of major biodiversity-related environmental-change categories expressed as a percentage of human-driven change (in red) relative to baseline (blue)</div></div></div>
<p>Rates of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">decline in biodiversity</a> in this sixth mass extinction match or exceed rates of loss in the five previous <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinction events</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Loss of biodiversity results in the loss of <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">natural capital</a> that supplies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem goods and services</a>. From the perspective of the method known as Natural Economy the economic value of 17 ecosystem services for Earth's <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> (calculated in 1997) has an estimated value of US$33 trillion (3.3x10<sup>13</sup>) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costanza97_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costanza97-224">[224]</a></sup> Species today are being wiped out at a rate 100 to 1,000 times higher than baseline, and the rate of extinctions is increasing. This process destroys the resilience and adaptability of life on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> The emergence of the sixth mass extinction is considered by conservation biologists, including <a href="/wiki/Rodolfo_Dirzo" title="Rodolfo Dirzo">Rodolfo Dirzo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a>, to be "one of the most critical manifestations of the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>" and the continued decline of biodiversity constitutes "an unprecedented threat" to the continued existence of human civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, a summary for policymakers of the largest, most comprehensive study to date of biodiversity and ecosystem services, the <i><a href="/wiki/Global_Assessment_Report_on_Biodiversity_and_Ecosystem_Services" title="Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services">Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services</a></i>, was published by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Science-Policy_Platform_on_Biodiversity_and_Ecosystem_Services" title="Intergovernmental Science-Policy Platform on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services">Intergovernmental Science-Policy Platform on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services</a> (IPBES). The main conclusions:
</p><p>1. Over the last 50 years, the state of nature has deteriorated at an unprecedented and accelerating rate.
</p><p>2. The main drivers of this deterioration have been changes in land and sea use, exploitation of living beings, climate change, pollution, and invasive species. These five drivers, in turn, are caused by societal behaviors, from consumption to governance.
</p><p>3. Damage to ecosystems undermines 35 of 44 selected UN targets, including the UN General Assembly's <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> for poverty, hunger, health, water, cities' climate, oceans, and land. It can cause problems with food, water and humanity's air supply.
</p><p>4. To fix the problem, humanity will need a transformative change, including <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>, reductions in <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> and waste, fishing quotas and collaborative water management. On page 8 the report proposes on page 8 of the summary " enabling visions of a good quality of life that do not entail ever-increasing material consumption" as one of the main measures. The report states that  "Some pathways chosen to achieve the goals related to energy, economic growth, industry and infrastructure and sustainable consumption and production (Sustainable Development Goals 7, 8, 9 and 12), as well as targets related to poverty, food security and cities (Sustainable Development Goals 1, 2 and 11), could have substantial positive or negative impacts on nature and therefore on the achievement of other Sustainable Development Goals".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>The October 2020 "Era of Pandemics" report by IPBES asserted that the same human activities which are the underlying drivers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_biodiversity_loss" title="Climate change and biodiversity loss">climate change and biodiversity loss</a> are also the same drivers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandemics" title="Pandemics">pandemics</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. Dr. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Daszak" title="Peter Daszak">Peter Daszak</a>, Chair of the IPBES workshop, said "there is no great mystery about the cause of the COVID-19 pandemic – or of any modern pandemic . . .
